The strengths:<br />- the plot is definitely well-thought off. Everything Erikson describes seems to have a purpose, big or small<br />- there are occasional moments of very fast-paced exciting writing when you can't seem to put the book down and you want to know what happens<br />That's it, can't really think of any more good things to say. There is no beauty that I could find,really.<br />- finally, there is too much random philosophizing. It is very boring and very obscure. Every single character is prone to have pages and pages of abstract \\"thoughts\\" whichare very difficult to follow and don't add anything to the book. I think getting rid of all this fluff, would have shortened the books by at least half, if not 2/3.<br />So, in conclusion, you can give the 1st book a shot. If you don't like it, don't force yourself--it's not going to get better! If you do like it, then definitely keep reading because all books are very similar in my opinion.

B00005ASHM|5.0|Pet Sounds is Brian Wilson's musical magnum opus.  Yet, it is doubtful Wilson's habitual use of hashish, accompanied by the occasional LSD stupor, would have permitted completion of Pet Sounds without the assistance and perseverance of lyricist Tony Asher.<br /><br />There would be precious few spurts of musical vitality from the Beach Boys after Pet Sounds.  Even the occasional innovative and appealing products, e.g Good Vibrations and Heroes and Villains, quickly dissipate into mindless refrains of overused choruses and gimmicky musical synthesis.<br /><br />Although there exists no lack of hop-heads who still acclaim the post-1966 Beach Boys catalogue, their praises are little more than attempts at validating their own descent into a concurrent degeneracy and consequent uselessness.  For example, what many would have others believe is \\"the soulful voice of Dennis Wilson\\" is actually attempts at vocalization through a thoroughly smoked and whiskey-soaked larynx and out a mouth pulled crooked by the drug-damaged portions of his brain.<br /><br />The Beach Boys never experienced the frustration of becoming musically irrelevant.  Instead, the essence of the Beach Boys, Brian Wilson, just plain old self-destructed.<br /><br />Pet Sounds is an exceptional listening experience throughout.  The more melancholy cuts are also very fitting, as they underscore the shame and sadness of the wonderful but rapidly dissipating talent of a 24-year-old Brian Wilson.

0439023521|4.0|I was excited to read this book, especially with all the hype surrounding it lately with the movie release. I wanted to know what all the fuss was about. So I dove right in.<br /><br />First, I'll be completely honest. The book started out extremely slow. The first couple chapters were interesting, inviting the reader into the idea quickly. But it trailed off quickly -- so quickly that I was bored to the point of almost putting it down at chapter 5. But I thought to myself, \\"There's so much hype, there must be something more.\\" So I skipped 5 chapters to chapter 10 and started skimming from there to see if I'd reached something of interest. Finally, the story was taking off again.<br /><br />The basis of the story is this: A poor society comprised of several districts ruled by a wealthy government are forced to compete in what come to be known as \\"The Hunger Games\\". The Games were started as punishment for an uprising the poor districts of the society waged against the government, but the governing body defeated the rebellion and crushed the poor under even heavier burdens.<br /><br />The rules of the hunger games were simple. Representatives were selected from each of the 12 societal districts, placed into an \\"arena\\", and forced to fight each other to the death. Last man (or woman) standing won food and riches for his (or her) family. But here's the catch: the combatants were mere teen-agers.<br /><br />No novel would be complete without a quasi-love story infused into it (because what good is blood and carnage among fighting children without a love story, right?). The combatants fight their way down to the last 2 remaining...uh...lovebirds.  And what good is a love story among blood and carnage waged by children against other children than a good old fashioned \\"Romeo and Juliet\\" scheme to wrap it all up?<br /><br />I will admit, the idea of the story was intriguing. People who don't know each other and who have done each other no wrong other than living in \\"opposing\\" districts are forced to fight to the death for the hopeful benefit of their respective families. It speaks rather loudly of the depravity of our human condition. Where does a person (or society) draw the line in the sand saying, \\"We will go this far and no farther!\\" ? When do we say, \\"We will not bow to the Establishment!\\" ?<br /><br />While the book raises some interesting societal issues (issues I'm sure someone could relate to war and greed and government in our day), I was disturbed the author chose the main players of the Games to be children. I wasn't offended, just disturbed. Maybe that's because we expect these kinds of stories with adult victims, not children. When we bring children into the equation, however, I think we are forced to think our positions through a bit further than we originally would have.<br /><br />What would you do if your family was in that situation?  Would you stand up and fight?  Or would you cower in fear?<br /><br />I give The Hunger Games 3 1/2 stars out of 5, primarily due to its predictability and slow start. The idea was intriguing, so it deserved at least 3 for provocation and thoughtfulness.<br /><br />You can see my other reviews at my blog site: christiancognition.blogspot.comg\\" districts are forced to fight to the death for the hopeful benefit of their respective families. Which makes Halestorm so freakin awesome. Groundbreaking-no. Quality music-yes.<br /><br />Lzzy Hale and her boys have put out a sophomore album that is much more varied than their previous efforts with tinges of southern rock, arena rock and even some pop overtones overlaid into their bread and butter hard rock. I think their branching out into different sounds really adds a level of complexity to their work. While they didn't get the mixture perfect on every song, the songs don't all sound the same and have much more personality. You won't find yourself thinking you accidentally hit the repeat button as each song is distinctly different which I really appreciate as that's a common problem within this genre. The inclusion of beautiful ballads like \\"Beautiful With You\\" or \\"Break In\\" make you appreciate that much more the hardness of other songs like \\"Love Bites.\\"<br /><br />The outstanding musicianship of the band is especially evident in the crafting of these songs. You'll find yourself waiting for a guitar breakdown or a quiet moment that helps convey emotion. Lzzy is simply an exceptional vocalist who can carry technical riffs, high notes and still convey heartbreaking vulnerability. This album also seems more personal than their last which showcases Lzzy's talent even more.<br /><br />This album is empowering, strong, soft, sexy and an insane amount of fun. It's system blaring, top down on a warm night kind of music. Highly recommend.<br /><br />**Like the others I also recommend getting the deluxe version. 0451196716|5.0|This was written in 1979 under Stephen King's \\"Richard Bachman\\" fake name.<br /><br />Within its genre, this is a legitimate classic and is King at his best. By genre, it is mass-market commercial suspense fiction - but it's great.<br /><br />This is also a chance to read King at the peak of his writing powers. He can still a good story, like \\"11/22/63,\\" but \\"Long Walk\\" is when he knew how to edit out his worst excesses. You can tell the effort and attention he paid to the very tightly controlled sentences, which I haven't seen in his longer fiction in quite some time.<br /><br />This isn't even a third the length of \\"11/22/63,\\" but it tells as interesting and as well-plotted a story, with characters the reader invests in and cares about. The young 'walkers' act like teenagers, too.<br /><br />With all the hoopla about \\"The Hunger Games,\\" this is a great example of an earlier story that dealt with a lot of the same issues - a dystopian society, a bizarre competition, and teenagers fighting and dying among themselves. It's brilliant - and very, very dark.

Her papa was a locksmith, so she learned the trade...<br />  * Stephen Henderson (\\"The Good Heart\\") is the long-time doorman who gives us a face and a heart that represent all the powerless victims of Wall Street.<br /><br />Combine a Macy's Thanksgiving Day parade, a cute little Brussels Griffon looking for a doggy treat, a funny script, great pacing, plus appealing stars, and you have an entertaining 104 minutes. B00004TA41|5.0|Don't let the pleasant album cover fool you, the music contained on this disc is not for the faint of heart. I admit that I owned \\"Interstellar Space\\" for over five years before I listened to it all the way through. Initially I found it to be too abrasive and would turn it off after a few minutes. However, when I was truly ready to experience what Coltrane laid down on these tracks it was a revelation. What Coltrane played here is technically impressive, and when listening to this album from that perspective one can't help but to be blown away. More important to me though is to step back and just listen. This was a man who did not have much time left to live, and to me it sounds like he was wringing everything he could out of the saxophone knowing that there was no time to waste. For the recording of \\"Interstellar Space\\" Coltrane stripped his band down to just himself and his drummer Rashied Ali, and this bare bones setting doesn't allow the listener's focus to stray very much. If that was Coltrane's intent, it was successful. While \\"Interstellar Space\\" is not for everyone, it is an important part of John Coltrane's legacy. He could have made a very comfortable living by playing standards for his entire career and doing what everyone wanted him to do. Instead, he pushed the boundaries until the day he died and lost many fans as a result. This is one of the reasons I admire the man so much. This was one of Coltrane's last albums, and I don't get the impression that he was ready to pack it in and walk into the sunset as the album cover implies. He went down swinging, and through the torrents of notes he recorded at these sessions I can always feel the turmoil of a man who still had rage enough to live but knew he wasn't going to. That's the mark of a transcendent album, and an incredible listening experience. Fans of John Coltrane should consider this an essential album to own.

Everyone has to work, what ever their physical health and if you fail to meet your work quota then you have your meagre rations cut. Public executions are mandatory attendance and Shins earliest memory is having been at one. He is the product of an `award marriage', and as such him and his brother will be born, live and die in the camp. They too will have to atone for the sins of the parents.<br /><br />He is taught to trust no one, to snitch on everyone and to be loyal only to the guards. The camps are the only places where photos of `The Dear Leader' are not compulsorily on display at all times - this is to re enforce that they are outside society. Hence their knowledge of the country, politics and even food is parlous to non existent. They are kept separate from the children of prisoners who have been on the outside. In short it is a living hell and then something happens that makes it even worse and is the catalyst for change in Shins pitiful life.<br /><br />This story has been told by Blaine Harden (Washington Post reporter) after numerous interviews with Shin. He has done a magnificent job or relaying the true story with amazing details and references for cross corroboration where ever possible. There is a really useful bibliography at the back for further reading. This is also an extremely accessible book and one which I found hard to not want to know more.<br /><br />It is split into three parts and the third dealing with trying to adapt to life as a free man and in many ways that is as hard as the actual escape for reasons that were a surprise to me. Blaine Harden has done extensive research for this and tried to keep as much of himself out of the book as possible, but that was always going to be difficult. Whilst I was totally absorbed with this book it was because of the story and not necessarily the writing, so even though I can highly recommend it is just not quite the five stars. Anyone interested in Korean history ought to read this at least once and I doubt even with fore knowledge of that regime that you will not be moved by Shin's story.into three parts and the third dealing with trying to adapt to life as a free man and in many ways that is as hard as the actual escape for reasons that were a surprise to me. B0000683DH|5.0|While fans may still (and likely always will) quibble about which of the franchise films is the best, the choices usually come down to three: the second, fourth, and sixth installments.  (Though I do think that part III is sometimes unfairly maligned.)<br /><br />\\"Wrath of Khan\\" gets my vote.  To my mind, \\"Wrath of Khan\\" contains nearly all of the best elements of `Star Trek': a perfectly balanced combination of adventure, wit, and drama.<br /><br />The interplay between the regular characters is spot-on here (chiefly between the lead trio of Kirk, Spock, and McCoy, although Scotty and Chekov also get some pretty good screen time), and for one of the first times, the franchise characters are allowed a fuller humanity.  The subplots surrounding Kirk's estranged family, advancing age, and stalled career aspirations (highlighted in a marvelous early dialogue scene between Kirk and McCoy) help to root the drama in a genuine human story.  This is important, as the villain (and his story) is so operatic.<br /><br />That last note - my use of the term `operatic' to describe Khan - isn't a criticism.  Ricardo Montalban imbues the role with enormous degrees of passion and theatricality.  Another performer might have come across as over the top or cartoonish, but Montalban somehow manages to avoid that trap, making Khan a passionate, human character.  A villain, certainly, but an interesting one, and one whose motives are worthy of driving the movie.<br /><br />There is a wonderful scene - and one moment, in particular - that unfolds about 2/3 of the way through the film, and it clearly displays the film's primary charms.  It's the scene set in the Genesis cave.  Kirk and a significant female character share a genuinely human conversation about the challenges of middle-age - something almost unthinkable in the old series.  It's a fine scene that accentuates the original series' emphasis on character interplay.  And then, a few minutes later, comes that moment: an exchange between Kirk and Khan - Khan on his spaceship, and Kirk still in the Genesis cave. (Fans of the movie will likely know precisely which moment I'm alluding to.)  Montalban and Shatner both play the scene with passion; but then Shatner takes it up a notch, in a moment of deliriously entertaining, Shatner-esque passion that encapsulates the genuinely sincere, occasionally cheesy charms of this series at its best.<br /><br />Note: if you get a chance, watch the Director's Cut.  There's not a huge difference between the two versions (and alas, the Director's Cut isn't available on Blu-Ray), but the few added minutes do help to flesh out and explain a subplot involving Scotty.ween Kirk and Khan - Khan on his spaceship, and Kirk still in the Genesis cave. 031617615X|2.0|I came to `Twilight' through the movies.  Intrigued, I decided to try the books and ended up firmly parked on the sofa as I devoured the series.  With the greater introspective detail, I liked Bella much more than in the films, and I looked forward to better understanding her insistence on continuing her pregnancy in `Breaking Dawn,' as well as Edward's immediate antipathy to the child.  Unfortunately, the book did not provide these details.  Bella is suddenly overcome by a need to have a baby.  In her own words: \\"I needed him (the baby) like the air I breathe.  Not a choice--a necessity.\\" Page 132. This was not a decision freely made after careful deliberation and consideration of options and consequences.  This was a compulsion.  Rather than having second thoughts as the pregnancy takes its toll on her, Bella holds firm to some indefinable belief that she will survive.  As disappointing as this revelation was, it was made worse by the fact that at no time does she acknowledge the risk to Edward.  She never seems to confront the terrible possibility that for one to live, the other might have to die.<br /><br />The book also leaves Edward's response unexplored. Why did he assume this unknown hybrid was a threat before there was any evidence that the pregnancy would endanger Bella's life?  Given the era in which his moral character was formed (and which required marriage prior to sex, which he insisted on), I wouldn't have expected Edward to favor abortion right away, but only after the danger to Bella was apparent. In a sense, his response is, like Bella's, more compulsion than actual choice.  Baby = danger= must do something.<br /><br />The whole crisis of the pregnancy doesn't bring Edward and Bella closer together or make them grow as characters.  Left wholly unexplored is the division it causes among the Cullen family.  The idea of a rift within their happy collective is fascinating and could have made a riveting plot twist under different circumstances.  But here, Meyer simply wanted a means to protect Bella from Edward's overzealous intentions, so she draws the lines and never looks back.  In reality, some of Bella's supporters might have reconsidered once they saw how the pregnancy threatened her, and by extension, Edward.  And surely someone would have brought up the lasting repercussions if Bella died.  Would the family have stayed together or would guilt and blame have split them apart?<br /><br />But this bleak scenario never materializes because everything works out in the end.  In fact, things work out a little too easily and conveniently.  Bella survives and begins her new existence as a vampire with none of the difficulties that readers had been led to expect.  Even if Bella's anticipation of the challenges made controlling her thirst easier, it would have been more satisfying if she'd had to work at it for a while.  She also seems to get the hang of her new strength pretty quickly.  I'd been looking forward to her accidentally ripping a few doors off their hinges or putting her foot through the car floor trying to brake.  If that wasn't enough, we also learn that she has a powerful ability that saves the day in the showdown with the Volturi.  It's all just a little too much.<br /><br />Renesmee turns out to be the perfect baby--no mention of poopy diapers, incessant crying, or waking up in the middle of the night to interrupt Bella and Edward's now risk-free expressions of affection.  And after the initial shock, Charlie takes the whole business of werewolves and vampires in stride.<br /><br />As for Jacob, he finally moves on--by imprinting on Renesmee.  Instead of working through his attachment to Bella and finally letting her go as a result of emotional growth, he simply transfers his devotion to her daughter. Now he can stay in Bella's life without feeling pain.  Judging from assorted comments in these reviews, this isn't the sort of solution that fans wanted for him, and I agree.<br /><br />We all want a happy ending for characters we've grown to love, but there is a satisfaction in watching them grow as we follow them through various struggles.  We don't see that happening in `Breaking Dawn.'  Instead, everyone stays the same while circumstances around them shift.  For this reviewer, it's a disappointing end to an otherwise enjoyable series.

0060920084|3.0|I got the feeling that while Bryson was writing this novel,  it was more about deadlines than a first hand, interested and intrepid observance of a great land; he didn't really want to be there, doing that.  For reasons known only to him, he had abandoned America for England awhile before, and perhaps whatever it was, became the underlying reason for his unenthusiastic return to homeland.  Ostensibly, he  had returned to take what he hoped would be a \\"Sentimental Journey\\", but wound up being dissatisfied, unamused, lonely.  Perhaps \\"lonely\\" describes his journey best, because not even Katz was there to drink beer with him; he had to drink alone when he could find a waitress who would bring him one after closing time without making him feel like a leper.  While traveling alone can be a boon in certain instances, it can also be a depressant, and it shows.  He drove at his own speed, coast to coast, seeing towns, countrysides and cities I would absolutely love to see for myself, but he seemed unimpressed by most of it.<br /><br />It's an enigma.  After having read \\"A Walk in The Woods\\" and \\"A sunburned Country\\", I dove into this one (\\"The Lost Continent\\" ) with abandon.  But while the first two books mentioned are peppered with a caustic, biting humor livening the travel narrative, which is just my cup of tea, I felt this one lacked a lot of something important; and while I enjoyed it to a degree, I felt rather flat after suffering through his coast to coast \\"missed adventure\\" with him.  As Flat as his descriptions of Nebraska or Kansas.  As \\"nothing\\" as his remarks about Highway 93 to Twin Falls, Idaho; where flat topped mesas born of volcanic fire give way to the beautiful Snake River that wends it's way through a black lava canyon of spectacular vistas, surrounded by magnificent mountains in the distance - none of which he apparently saw, or if he did, neglected to write of it.  The West is a country of vast distances; to travel through at high speed with the sole intent of \\"getting it over with\\" is surely a way to miss most of it.<br /><br />But his disrespect of the East, all the way from Georgia to Vermont, was evident too; instead of using his daring wit as a condiment to the tale as it had been in books past, it became narcissistic in this one; one can't help but feel that he had personal issues clogging his views, dragging him down with them as he sneered his way across country.  In many instances, you knew exactly what he was telling was hard truth; that's always welcome - but the tempering journalistic enthusiasm so evident in prior books was struggling painfully, doggedly, trying to break the surface and never quite accomplished it.<br /><br />I left Bryson after spending a lot of time with him in the pages of this book, but he wasn't quite the same storyteller I tagged along with through the woods on the Appalachian Trail, or in the back country of Australia.  I have mixed emotions about how to rank it; but even though I came away with the feelings I did, I would recommend it to others because there was a lot of worthwhile humor in it if you are forewarned to sidestep the depression.  While I was late to the Bryson party (I always am) I have high hopes for his next book; I know he has another good one in him.

He has to move from Tokyo back to his small hometown, where his mother left him a house, and he finds work as a  \\"Nokanshi,\\" which is a person who prepares the dead person for the journey onwards. They're kind of like morticians only they do the work in front of the deceased person's family. He keeps his job secret from his wife because it's a shameful occupation, since they basically make money off the dead, and he's haunted by his hatred for his father who abandoned him a long time ago. His wife finds out about the job and wants him to quit and find something else. Towards the end, Daigo finally understands that his position is not shameful, along with others who ostracized him when they found out about his job, including his wife, and they realize that he performs a function that not only helps the grieving families, but also gives dignity to the dead. He's also able to make peace with his father -- when he's faced with the father's death -- and Daigo finally embraces change, which then allows him to fully experience life.<br /><br />The movie is beautifully shot and the soundtrack is gorgeous with the Cello its main instrument. Such people are absent in this book.<br /><br />I wound up wondering why the book presented such a bleak world with so much violence, so dispassionately. 1250008409|5.0|I needed Andy Puddicombe's book because I wanted to break out of What If? and Coulda-Shoulda-Woulda-Land.  I want to live in the moment and enjoy or at least be present in my everyday life.  The happiest times in my life have always been when I'm completely present.  Remember what it was like to fall in love?  To land in a new city on vacation?  To hear a symphony in person for the first time?  Unfortunately, it seems the longer I live, the more thoughts and concerns that I have and the more difficult it is to stop all the \\"what if and could-shoulda-woulda\\" stuff that is buzzing around in my head.  What happened to the days when everything was new and exciting and an adventure?  What happened to the days when I didn't worry about every little thing and just concentrated on the present?  I've read other books on meditation and mindfulness and I've tried, I really have, to clear my mind and just be present.  But telling myself not to think of anything is like telling myself not to think of a purple cow.  The more I tried, the more purple cows that appeared.<br /><br />Andy Puddicombe's book, \\"Get Some Headspace\\" has finally got me going in the right direction.  I'm not totally there yet, but I'm making progress.  The thing I liked best about his book is that he's very down to earth and has a great way of explaining how to get to a point of living mindfully.  He uses lots of stories and analogies.  One of my favorites is the Blue Sky analogy.  Think of being in a storm with dark clouds everywhere in sight and rain and thunder and lightning and noise.  Then think of taking off in an airplane and breaking through all those dark clouds to a sunny blue sky.  The blue sky is like the calm state our minds realize when we meditate.  It may take a little work to get there, but it always is and always was there.  It's just a matter of lifting through all the commotion and noise below.  Andy proposes a way to get that \\"lift\\" that will break through the clouds in a way that is not overwhelming.  His Take 10 program proposes taking just 10 minutes a day to calm your mind.  Rather than trying to not think of all the clouds and thunder and lightning he tells us just to sit back and acknowledge them.  To me that's kind of like sitting in the plane and watching the clouds and watching them fall away behind as the blue sky becomes visible.<br /><br />The book isn't about mantras or chants or connecting with some mysterious force.  While I was waiting for the book to ship I did a search on the title and found that the author has a website that has all kinds of information and 10 minute guided meditations to help you find that calm place and get some headspace.  You can listen to 10 days worth of these guided meditations plus more online content that the book gives you access to.  If you want to continue these guided meditations you can sign up, for a fee, for a month, year, or longer membership that will give you complete access.  The book covers all of this information that you'll get in the free section plus more.  This book is not an advertisement for the web content, like some books I've read in the past.  He gives you all the tools you'll need to get you on the path to meditation and mindfulness in the book.<br /><br />Although the book is only 224 pages (for the advance reading copy) he does a great job of explaining the principles of meditation.  In fact, he is often redundant and could probably have written it in a lot less words.  But if you're like me, with purple cows running rampant through your brain, redundancy can be a good thing.  I needed to hear this message in stories, in analogies and in plain old language to fully grasp just where I'm going and why it's taken me so long to do a seemingly simple thing.  I needed to be convinced.  It wasn't even simple for the author in the beginning. Andy shares in his book that he ran off to Asia and trained as a Buddist monk but he went through numerous monasteries looking to find his way. Hehad the same problems we all have with trying to be present in the moment so he knows how to explain the process. His approach in this book isn't religious and if you watch a video of him on his website he doesn't even look like a monk dressed in a plaid shirt rather than orange robes. And he has a very friendly, easy going manner.<br /><br />I wanted to mention that the guided meditations on the Andy's website have been a great help to me so far and I may subscribe when I finish the free content.  In some ways they seem a bit like hypnosis in that they're very relaxing and his voice is energizing and soothing at the same time.  It helps to chase away the purple cows and all the what-if and could-shoulda-woulda stuff and most importantly, it gets me to a calm place rather quickly.  The nice thing is that the guided meditations are available as a smartphone app so I can listen on my iPhone so I can be anywhere, not tied to sitting at my computer.<br /><br />This book may not be for everyone, but it's been a great help to me.had the same problems we all have with trying to be present in the moment so he knows how to explain the process. His approach in this book isn't religious and if you watch a video of him on his website he doesn't even look like a monk dressed in a plaid shirt rather than orange robes. And he has a very friendly, easy going manner.<br /><br />I wanted to mention that the guided meditations on the Andy's website have been a great help to me so far and I may subscribe when I finish the free content.  In some ways they seem a bit like hypnosis in that they're very relaxing and his voice is energizing and soothing at the same time.  It helps to chase away the purple cows and all the what-if and could-shoulda-woulda stuff and most importantly, it gets me to a calm place rather quickly.  The nice thing is that the guided meditations are available as a smartphone app so I can listen on my iPhone so I can be anywhere, not tied to sitting at my computer.<br /><br />This book may not be for everyone, but it's been a great help to me.

The bonus tracks on \\"Surrender\\" (Baby I'll Come) and \\"Everything Is Everything\\" (Wish I Knew, What Are You Doing The Rest Of Your Life, and Something) were more exciting and I listen to those tracks constantly.  Perhaps my favorite Ross period is the early 70s?<br /><br />The final disappointment is the last track (track 15) on Disc 2: a 15 minute and 50 second interview with Ross.  I doubt even the hard core fans will listen to that more than once.  I would have rather had 2 extra bonus tracks and no interview.<br /><br />I've played this Special Edition only twice and filed it away.  Final Grade: C. B00701QVVY|5.0|The thrash revival, or more accurately, the second generation of thrashers that have risen in the shadows of greats like Slayer, Metallica (at least the first three albums) and Exodus features lots of hot young bands full of energy and chops, like Evile and Havok, for example.  But it's also interesting to note that the original generation of thrashers in many cases are putting out the strongest albums of their careers, as if to show the youngsters just who exactly pioneered this form of metal in the first place, and who can still blow those whippersnappers off the stage.<br />  No band embodies that description better than the mighty Overkill, who have released great thrash albums for three decades plus and if anything are getting more brutal with each new release.<br />  \\"The Electric Age\\" follows on the footsteps of their fantastic \\"Ironbound\\" album, an overlooked and under appreciated masterpiece from these NJ-NY titans.  Overkill fans and smart critics knew two things:  how good \\"Ironbound\\" was, and how there was little doubt these guys could follow up with an equally rocking new album.  Unlike some bands who have trouble following up legendary albums, it's no sweat for these guys on their 16th album.<br />  Bobby \\"Blitz\\" Ellsworth remains a great thrash singer, full of chops and a fine lyricist to boot.  D.D. Verni, his long time partner in crime on bass, composes the music and as always, comes up with the goods.  \\"Electric Rattlesnake\\" is a fine heavy hitter of a single.  \\"Black Daze\\" recalls some of the bluesy rhythms of earlier times without sounding one bit wimpy and \\"Save Yourself\\" follows in the vein of \\"Bring On The Night\\" from \\"Ironbound\\".<br />  Guitarists Derek Tailor and Dave Linsk are punishing riff masters, with Linsk providing his usual killer lead work.  Verni locks in with Ron Lipnicki's furious drumming, and the overall result is thrash that demands your attention and respect.  The production shines and Overkill show once again why they should have been one of the Big Four instead of a few other bands.  They've certainly put out great consistent albums without the long dry spells, millions of personnel changes and strange forays into \\"Lulu\\" land.  \\"The Electric Age\\" may not get the attention it deserves, but you can rectify that by picking it up or ordering it and help Overkill take its rightful place as thrash titans in the minds of all metal fans, not just their die hard fans.have been one of the Big Four instead of a few other bands. It seems like some reviewers have a reverse prejudice here--they should try to be as open to this author and her story as the inmates were.  She IS pretty and she IS smart and she IS well educated, but to me it didn't feel like she was tooting that horn so much as acknowledging that she was aware of the differences between her and many of her friends in prison.  If anything, having the background she did probably made it harder for her to be accepted by her inmates, who fortunately saw her for the person she was, and did.  Over and over throughout the story I got the impression she thought she was fortunate AND that she WAS repentant. B0062NAX1U|1.0|This movie is such a disaster, I don't even know where to begin. It has no focus, it's slow, there's no main character, the acting is wooden, the plot just sort of dissolves, the production looks and feels cheap. Even the spiritual messages are all over the map. Worst of all, it's really really boring.<br /><br />It starts ok. A new cop joins a small town police force and for a while he's the de facto protagonist. I figured we the audience would get to follow and learn with him as he struggled to adjust to his new environment and job, but the movie quickly loses track. There's a surprisingly well done chase scene, the other cops  get a cursory  introduction and the writer sort of hints that a local gang chapter will become the antagonist. Then the movie  forgets  all about the rookie cop for a while and  just sort of meanders around  as it explains all the different officers' troubled family dynamics. It's a little unevenly paced, but so far it's not too far off from regular Katheryn Bigelow-style cop drama. There's some Christian messages worked into the script fairly organically. The police chief overtly stating the movie's main thesis was kind of clumsy, but it wasn't too offensive. It's a pretty dry sequence though, (a hint of things to come)  so they throw in another chase to pick up the movie's energy but this one doesn't work as well. The \\"slingshot maneuver\\", wherein a speeding police car whips the tail around so the officer sitting in the back seat can leap out the door ,taking advantage of the cars centrifugal forge, made my eyes involuntarily roll. I don't care if that's a real thing police officers do. The way they shot it was cheesy and it looks stupid. It's made worse by the fact that it's the last boost of energy the movie gives us before spiraling into melancholy.<br /><br />Around this time we are introduced to Javier, a Mexican day laborer. He is poor and desperate and out of work. He can only afford a tortilla for lunch. But he is a good Christianand he trusts that the lord will find him some work. At the same time he's out looking for work, one of the officers mentions to his colleagues that he's building a shed and needs a worker. One of the officers recommends someone also named Javier, but not the Javier we have been introduced to. By lucky coincidence/divine intervention, Javier 2 doesn't show up and the first Javier happens to be walking by, praying for help. The officer, mistaking him for Javier 2, calls him by name to help build a shed. This leads to the stupidest, laziest \\"Who's on First\\" ripoff I've ever seen.  I'm paraphrasing but it's something like \\"My friend just told me Javier couldn't make it. Who are you?\\" \\"I'm Javier\\" \\"No Javier couldn't make it! Who are you?\\" \\"I'm Javier\\"  Its just painful. It's like watching wit itself die before your eyes.<br /><br />I guess around here the writer noticed that nothing was happening and he needed something to kick the movie into gear so in comes the 9-11 moment where a horrible incident galvanizes the cops into action. I thought maybe the wise old chief would be killed by the gang members so the cops would have to step up and lead themselves. Maybe they could rehabilitate the bad guys with their christian message or something? No,the film makers instead go the opposite direction. A character we have only seen once before is killed off camera in a car accident. At this point the movie comes to such an abrupt stop it felt like getting flung through a windshield. The movie loses all pretenses of storytelling and falls into a solid hour or so of grief counceling.  It's characters crying, people assuring them that God has a plan and soft piano music for what feels like forever. I don't know why they thought this would make for good popcorn entertainment. I guess one of the film makers wandered into a funeral home and said to himself \\"Now THIS is entertainment! This is what audiences want\\".<br /><br />The worst part is that they cap it with a joke.  Seriously.I've been sitting there for an hour trying to let the filmmakers establish a tone that manipulates me into feeling sad but they end it by having the grieving father do a call back joke wherein he accidentally ends a phone conversation to his boss with \\"I love you\\". The movie is completely tone deaf. We go from crying about a dead child to wacky sitcom fare in under a minute. It's jarring and confusing and symptomatic of the biggest problem this movie faces: it has no idea what it wants to be. The tone and the pacing are in such a wild flux it feels like four or five movies stitched together like Frankenstein. Theres a cop movie sewn to an ABC Family drama stitched to a \\"Coping with Loss\\" self help tape, combined with a \\"purity ring\\" sermon, the worst sitcoms you've ever seen and just a dash of \\"The Shield\\"<br /><br />So the upshot of the extended grief sequence is that the grieving father ( who's suddenly  the main character for the rest of the film) comes up with a radical pledge that he will be a good Christian leader for  his family. All the other cops and Javier take the pledge too.  This leads to a strange purity ring segment that feels completely out of context.  The scene where the rookie cop takes his daughter to a fancy restaurant and proposes to keep her virginity until the lord shows him a man worth giving her away to is bizarre. If you're going to shoehorn in that sort of message you have to be careful with how you handle it, and they completely drop the ball.  It feels out of place and creepy.<br /><br />Next up comes the ending from a completely different movie. The film makers cut back to the gang members here and there throughout the movie to remind you that they still exist. They'll show them hanging out in abandoned warehouses or beating up a kid to initiate him into the gang but nothing ever really comes of it. Theres no comeuppance or confrontation. They're just sort of set aside in favor of a new bad guy.  It's never been mentioned before the last half hour of the movie but someone has been stealing evidence. The cops do some sleuthing and figure out its one of their close cop friends. The movie tries to express a conflict, because turning in the cop will tarnish the reputation of their precinct but they don't really seem to struggle with it at all and the fallout of their decision is never shown. Instead they agree to watch after the disgraced cops family for him and send him to a federal prison, which may as well be a death sentence for a cop, but there's no follow up so who cares? Afterwards they have a picnic and the grieving father holds a toast challenging the officers to be moral Christian leaders.  He's not looking at the camera but he's talking directly to the audience like Ferris Bueller. I get that theyr'e trying to leave the audience with some sort of message but the dialogue is so stilted it just rips you out of what's left of the story right before credits roll. May as well have smacked him over the head with a boom mic.<br /><br />I really want to like this movie but I can't.  A straightforward movie about cop drama laced in Christian allegory couldve been really good but this movie is too pretentious, lazy and bloated. The movie treats you like you're an idiot, and leaves nothing for you to figure out or interpret. Everything is painted in broad strokes. The messages are tenuously tied to what can laughingly be called the plot and treated with such heavy handedness it felt at times like it was scolding me. There's no subtlety. The humor is stale, uncomfortable and  stingily doled out.  Looking at it from an objective storytelling point of view, it's a complete failure. It's like the story was an afterthought. There are so many incomplete arcs and unresolved issues it makes me wonder if they even had a script when they started filming. If you want a strong Christian message and nothing else, this is your film. If you care about things like plot or story or character development stay far faraway. This movie is not for you.

0439023491|2.0|The end of The Hunger Games was promising, leaving a love triangle, with Katniss at its center, and a brewing revolution among the districts unresolved. Catching Fire took these finely woven threads and tied them into an incoherent, ponderous knot.<br /><br />Due to the book's limited, first-person perspective, large-scale events, like the revolution, can only be hinted at, talked about, and related third hand. Imagine a book about the Iraq War told by the Washington Post Pentagon reporter. Wouldn't you rather hear the story from the reporter embedded with expeditionary forces?<br /><br />The victory tour temporarily resolves this problem, as Katniss and Peeta get to see the oppressive living conditions in the other districts and gauge the revolutionary mood of the people. Afterwards, whispers of discontent and fleeting news reports become gossip fodder for whole chapters. District 12's submission to a Capitol crackdown brings the situation to a simmer, but author Suzanne Collins shies away from showing us the flashpoint, the \\"catching fire,\\" if you will. Whatever happens, it is always happening somewhere else.<br /><br />Katniss spends much of the book in hysterics over her presumptuous commitment to protect everyone in her close-knit circle, keeping up pretenses for the Capitol, juggling two fake romances, and helping along the revolution, all at the same time. When she is--yawn--summoned back to the arena for the second year in a row, her mind is always on something other than the arena action, which might be this book's saving grace.<br /><br />The arena action is so rushed and cartoonish that the reader quickly becomes desensitized. Collins throws in everything but the kitchen sink: seizure-inducing fog, flesh-eating monkeys, tsunamis, etc. Pharaoh had it easy compared to these tributes. The arena in The Hunger Games, by contrast, had depth and felt real. As the reader you absorbed it, marinated in it, rather than had it forced on you.<br /><br />All of Katniss' juvenile babblings, preoccupations, and forced logical deductions are for naught. Because she is such a small fish and an emotional tumble dryer, the people in the know don't entrust her with any information until the last chapter, wherein a nauseating bit of exposition explains everything neither she nor the reader could have possibly figured out on his own.<br /><br />The end result is a book in which the stakes couldn't be higher, but the shots are being called elsewhere, while the \\"heroine\\" stumbles around thinking she has an effect on the outcome. What a waste of time.<br /><br />The best part of the book is the development of Peeta as a character. We see he has other emotions than caring for Katniss. He reacts angrily when Katniss and Haymitch reveal a secret they were keeping from him (one of several Collins engineered to artificially maintain the tension). It's good to know he's not the complete sissy the first book made him out to be. Gale by contrast is as duplicitous as Katniss. One minute he wants to flee into the forest, the next he wants to fight the good fight. Count me in Peeta's corner, if you must, but I won`t be reading the third book to see who Katniss finally choses.' juvenile babblings, preoccupations, and forced logical deductions are for naught. 0439023521|2.0|Anyone who sits down to write a book has my admiration. Also, I fully believe that Collins is a talented writer who is bringing to light stories and topics that need our attention. I appreciate her work and vision and perseverance.<br /><br />Unfortunately \\"The Hunger Games\\" series leave me unnourished.<br /><br />The first book in the series was promising, especially as it carried a trenchant critique of our making a circus of death/survival. In an interview I read Collins expressed being concerned about sending our young people - teenagers - to war and what that does to them and also wondering why people enjoyed the \\"Survivor\\" TV show. However the book quickly lost focus and the narrative devolved into something formulaic, violence oriented and without a sense of having learned something. The story was stretched thin (probably so it could last three volumes), became increasingly violent and, worse of all, eviscerated all agency and personality from the initially promising female protagonist whose focus came down to decide between two male love interests. All in all, one of the most disappointing book experiences of my life. Mainly because it started with enormous promise (which was lost even before the first book ended). I'm happy to know that a writer has experienced fame and fortune, but it's at the price of the story that could have been told, a story that we need.<br /><br />Interestingly, I had a similar experience with her \\"Gregor the Overlander\\" series. An utterly fascinating premise and a strong first book. Even the subsequent books in the series have moments of brilliance. However, yet again, she opted for the easy road - increased battles (instead of finding other, more nuanced, ways to evolve relationships/solve conflicts), the assignment of villainy (troublingly, mainly to the women characters) and the outcome in which no growth or much insight was achieved.<br /><br />At the end of the series (I only skimmed the last book - just as I only skimmed thelast two books in the \\"Hunger Games\\" series) I was disappointed that all that obvious intellect and storytelling talent ended with very little to show. Then again, I thought, perhaps that's Collins' point. Namely, you have a war and think it'll be the answer when in fact, at the end, there really isn't anything grand at all - no answer or improvement just damage and sadness. So, even though I wasn't happy with it I had to acknowledge that it was a viable choice. By \\"viable\\" I mean I could see it as emerging from/organic to the story she told.<br /><br />However when she did it again in the \\"Hunger Games\\" series I was annoyed. After all, it's easy to criticize a society, it's easy to depict conflict and battle (after all, we have generations of stories and books and movies doing that). But unless we find ways to make interesting stories about peace, nonviolent conflict and less-than-perfect-but-workable solutions; unless we make those stories as compelling as the old good vs. evil narrative we'll never be able to imagine, much less live out, a different way of being. I consider it a failure of imagination and narrative laziness to not push oneself beyond the script of battle-victory/hero(ine)-end of story.<br /><br />Our young people need new stories - the human race needs new stories.

B0071XO8RA|4.0|I became interested in Wool from reading Hugh Howey's posts on Kindleboards and vicariously enjoying his incredible success with the series. I launched into the first book with gusto. Howey's prose is clear, smooth and evocative. Anyone worried that Wool is less than professional in writing or presentation needn't be. I've read plenty of traditionally published books that aren't as well written or formatted.<br /><br />The first book presents us with a world in which the last survivors of some kind of holocaust live in a 134-floor silo, protected from a natural environment that has become wildly toxic. The concept is great and Howey makes the most of it, providing a number of twists that regularly notch up the suspense.<br /><br />Howey's main strength, though, is in his ability to create well-rounded characters whom we care about deeply and immediately. In this regard, I think he's actually better than some of the s-f giants of the past, such as (because someone else made the comparison) Isaac Asimov, whose work I positively devoured in my youth but whose characters were rarely more than two-dimensional cutouts moved around to serve the plot. (And if there is anything more cringe-worthy than a \\"classic\\" s-f writer's attempt at flirtatious dialogue, I don't know what it would be, unless it's listening to an elementary school orchestra. But I digress.) Howey writes real people who would be at home in any \\"literary\\" novel, which is a particularly high achievement in the s-f world.<br /><br />So, why just four stars, aside from the fact that I'm a tough grader?<br /><br />SPOILERS AHEAD<br /><br />Some things about the concept I just didn't buy. I'll take a few \\"givens\\" in s-f, of course, but ultimately s-f requires a willing suspension of disbelief that I never quite achieved with Wool.<br /><br />For instance, the physical action hinges on the central stairwell that characters must travel...laboriously. I'm sorry, but I didn't buy that there were no elevators. I get that electrical power is limited, and I get that the overlords (my corny term, not Howey's!) don't really want people socializing and communicating, but...no 134-floor structure is going to function without elevators. You need a freight elevator. NEED it. Absolutely no one would design and build a 134-floor silo without an elevator for moving heavy stuff.<br /><br />And there will always be executives who demand an elevator. The aged mayor having to hike down 130 floors, and back up again? Nope, didn't buy it. The evil, powerful guy would have had a secret elevator. There would have been an elevator for emergencies, if only a crude people-mover that people stand on. The mechanics would have worked up something for themselves, maybe disguising it as something else.<br /><br />The cleaning bothered me, too. Keeping the lenses clean really isn't that big a deal. There would be a way to remove them from inside to clean them, or send someone out briefly and bring them back in, or something. Again, I get the sociological imperative of the cleaning ritual/punishment, but I didn't entirely buy it as a method anyone would come up with to keep a populace in check.<br /><br />So, these are pretty big pills that I just didn't swallow. Still, I gave them to Howey as the price of admission to his world. There were a couple of other problems, though.<br /><br />MORE SPOILERS<br /><br />I really had a problem with the poisoning. Poison is slipped into someone's canteen (an important person), and there is virtually no investigation into the incident. The poisoning could only happen in one place, and a limited number of people had access to the canteen at this time. This crime wouldn't have kept a real-world cop busy for a full day, but Howey's sheriff doesn't, apparently, launch even the most basic inquiry.<br /><br />I can understand if Howey didn't want to sidetrack his story with a murder investigation, but I felt that he really gave it short shrift.<br /><br />The main problem Ihad with Wool, though, came with the last two books, when the pacing ground to a snail's pace.<br /><br />Jack London wrote a killer short story called \\"To Build a Fire,\\" detailing a man's effort to build a fire in the wilderness to keep away wolves. What works brilliantly in a short story gets really tiresome in a novel. With Wool 4 and 5, the minutia began gathering underfoot until this exciting read became a slog. I'll admit, I started speed-reading. Normally I like to savor a book, and Howey's writing is worth savoring, but Wool 5 in particular became tedious, as did the back-and-forth between our main character's story and the story of a secondary character who was far less fascinating.<br /><br />So, ultimately, I have to give the Wool Omnibus a four-star rating for a great start and a less stellar finish.<br /><br />I have every confidence that Hugh Howey will continue to make a name for himself and look forward to his future works!had with Wool, though, came with the last two books, when the pacing ground to a snail's pace.<br /><br />Jack London wrote a killer short story called \\"To Build a Fire,\\" detailing a man's effort to build a fire in the wilderness to keep away wolves. What works brilliantly in a short story gets really tiresome in a novel. With Wool 4 and 5, the minutia began gathering underfoot until this exciting read became a slog. I'll admit, I started speed-reading. Normally I like to savor a book, and Howey's writing is worth savoring, but Wool 5 in particular became tedious, as did the back-and-forth between our main character's story and the story of a secondary character who was far less fascinating.<br /><br />So, ultimately, I have to give the Wool Omnibus a four-star rating for a great start and a less stellar finish.<br /><br />I have every confidence that Hugh Howey will continue to make a name for himself and look forward to his future works!

B006R1T40I|4.0|\\"Slipstream\\" is first and foremost the story of an artist returning to her realm after a long dry spell, rediscovering her fountain of inspiration and acquiring the drive to once again practice her craft. Since that artist happens to be Bonnie Raitt, one of America's most iconic and important musicians, it comes as no surprise that it is an enlightening, hearty listen with a feels-like-home comfortability.<br /><br />The seven-year gap between \\"Slipstream\\" and 2005's terrific \\"Souls Alike\\" is the longest Raitt has taken between studio efforts, understandable since her life in the interim  has been fraught with loss - of parents, of brother, of best friend. While that certainly can be read as informing her soul-searching take on Bob Dylan's masterpiece \\"Standing in the Doorway\\" and the aching piano/vocal \\"God Only Knows,\\" the album only occasionally dips its toe into the deep end of introspection. In fact, the only tune Raitt co-writes here is the rambunctious, sing-songy \\"Down to You.\\"<br /><br />This is music with a joyful edge, crafted for the simple pleasure of craft (an artist of Raitt's stature taking such a long gap between records indicates a lack of prioritizing sales or hits) and therefore of great interest to her longtime devotees. It doesn't have the radio-friendly edge of earlier recordings, and it need not. A few songs have a similar bite, especially at the core of the LP, but listeners on the market for a new Raitt album - and there are more than a few of them - will certainly be pleased.<br /><br />Randall Bramlett's \\"Used to Rule the World\\" kicks off the album with funky, slamming, kinetic instrumentation. Raitt's playing is prominent, large and boisterous, firmly establishing that time has not frayed her resolve. Similar can be said of Paul Brady and Michael O'Keefe's witty, biting \\"Marriage Made in Hollywood,\\" which Raitt wraps her voice around with cool, supple assurance, as well as \\"Right Down the Line\\" with its Caribbean flourishes and sure-footed percussion.<br /><br />The meditative, silky \\"Take My Love With You\\" and \\"Not Cause I Wanted To\\" showcase Raitt's elegant, quiet passion as a vocalist. The intimate, unobtrusive nature of the recording allows the songs to furrow into the mind and summon repeated listens. This is music that breathes, as the lady behind it surely intended.<br /><br />With \\"Slipstream\\" Raitt resurfaces in radiant, surefooted fashion, confident in her abilities, hungry to record new tunes and hit the road to play them for audiences. After 40 years in the business she retains a disproportionate amount of soul, vigor and longing. Listeners are blessed to have her.ed percussion.<br /><br />The meditative, silky \\"Take My Love With You\\" and \\"Not Cause I Wanted To\\" showcase Raitt's elegant, quiet passion as a vocalist. The intimate, unobtrusive nature of the recording allows the songs to furrow into the mind and summon repeated listens. This is music that breathes, as the lady behind it surely intended.<br /><br />With \\"Slipstream\\" Raitt resurfaces in radiant, surefooted fashion, confident in her abilities, hungry to record new tunes and hit the road to play them for audiences. After 40 years in the business she retains a disproportionate amount of soul, vigor and longing. Listeners are blessed to have her.

0439023513|2.0|A woman friend of mine suggested a month ago I read the Hunger Games books. Not my usual type of reading material but I plunged away and found the first two books excellent and got me hooked...surprisingly. I didn't think these were actually Y/A material, but whatever. Naturally I had to finish the series and read the last book...Mockingjay. Oops, this one wasn't as good and was much darker.<br /><br />As I am an adult male and have read my fair share of darker, more psychological themed novels I shouldn't have been too shocked by Miss Collins book, but I kinda was. I didn't care for it that much and I tried to reason out why. It wasn't as though the first two books were Wizard of Oz stuff..they were pretty grim in their own right. But Katniss and the other characters were so well developed and the story was gripping, and definitely holds you attention.<br /><br />So what went wrong?<br /><br />First: The first person narrative that had worked well in the first two books, didn't serve this last book and the story as well. Too many things were happening away from Katniss that needed more development. Her presence being required to relate the action wasn't always needed or desirable as much as before.<br /><br />Second: Katniss seemed to be a situational lover which became very noticable in MJ. She starts out as a best friend with Gale, but not romantically. She loves him, but more as a companion, confident, and perhaps a substitute for her recently passed father. When she volunteers to be the tribute from District 12 she and Haymitch play up a romance/lovers situation with an unsuspecting, but very nice guy, Peeta. He learns about the strategy after the two escaped as winners. He finds out that it was 'not real.' However, readers can ask themselves a question, was Katniss pretending by the end of Hunger Games or not? It wasn't quite clear as the girl frequently questions her romantic feelings...she can be very contradictory. When they eventually return home she turnsmore of her attention to Gale Hawthorne and shows her deep feelings for him after his whipping. Peeta now realizes that maybe she loves Gale. Okay, that's possible...but I don't think so. The major story arc is the developing resistance movement against the Capitol. But the romance between Peeta and Katniss is the catalyst for the potential revolution...hence its importance. The romance and the revolution have become intertwined. By the end of Catching Fire, Collins leaves no doubt that Katniss is indeed in love with Peeta....even Snow is convinced.<br /><br />And now we get to MJ. Here is Peeta being tortured and enduring severe chemical and physical mind alteration techniques linking Katniss and the Muttations, so that when he see's one he sees the other. In the meantime, Gale is stalking her all over her newly situated housing in District 13. He is using this opportunity to win her back, or just win her. In almost every scene Gale is sitting next to her and being helpful. They grow close again. They hunt together again...while Peeta screams. Coin asks Katniss if she wants Gale to be announced as her new lover. She is shocked. When Peeta is \\"rescued\\" (he was really allowed to escape by Snow) he now becomes the Manchurian Candidate...programmed to kill Mockingjay...which he attempts. She becomes angry, confused, shocked and despairing, and now attaches herself even more to Gale. She begins to be more affectionate and intimate with him. So Collins has now reversed what was known before and restarted the triangle. When she sees Peeta the next time he confronts her with the Gale rumors about them, and she gets offended and she throws her hugging and Kissing of Gale back in Peeta's face, which unleashes his one and only nasty remark about her loyalty. Things are getting messy. I think coin may be enjoying Katniss's reputational fallout a bit. Prim then tells her that Peeta has been through alot, and Haymitch basically says what if the situation were reversed, what would he be doing for you? So now we have the great heroine from the first two books seemingly falling in love with the one available guy. This diminishes her in the heads of the readers and IMO somewhat weakens her character. If this Was Collins intent...it worked.<br /><br />Third: The commando raid to kill Snow was unnecessary as the rebels were already preparing an invasion. And why would they risk good soldiers for an almost suicide mission? Having Katniss train for combat seemed more like her attempt to forget about Peeta...and then he comes along for the ride anyway....handcuffed most of time and asking to be killed for the rest of it. Finnick is killed so quickly and off screen, you're not sure at first it happened. A bit too fast and anti-climactic for a great character. Prim is sent in as a medic to the combat area and is killed by a cluster bomb, built by Beetee from a design suggestion from Gale. He was blamed by Katniss for complicity in Prim's death and lets him go...another weak part of the story. He was no more to blame for her death than an auto engineer for someone's car accident...but Collins needed a way to dispose of Gale's involvement in Katniss because she HAS to eventually be with Peeta at the end or the whole story from the very begining would collapse. But this was a cheap way for Katniss to end her relationship with him. He at least deserved to be told that she really loved Peeta. Katniss later votes for a Capitol kids Hunger Game, why is not clear. Snow tells her that it wasn't he that ordered that fatal attack on Prim and other women and children. She goes to ask Haymitch and all he can do is ask her how her love life is going. She stalks out without asking. All this has long passed intense drama and become more melodrama...sad to say.<br /><br />Fourth\\" Katniss kills Coin instead of Snow...1000 pages she's been talking about killing him but then kills someone else instead...but Coin did deserve it. Katniss has a complete mental breakdown,found not guilty of murder by reason of insanity, and sent to Botany Bay, aka District 12. War is hell. All alone in her house tended to only by Greasy, Katniss, acting very much like her mother after her father died, is just marking time. She asks Greasy if she heard anything about Gale, and was informed of where he was. She has a moment of reflection for the years they were friends and then lets it go. Peeta then quietly shows up planting primrose, they start spending more time together, then sleeping together again, kissing again, and they fall in love again...or just finally remembered they already were. But Peeta's reuniting with Katniss takes only about two or three paragraphs...now that's economizing. It was out of proper perspective. Collins devotes a great deal more time to Katniss's state of depression at home than on any recovery. We have already experienced the many times throughout the book that she has been severely hurt and traumatized. Now at the very end, this seems like Miss Collins is just piling it on without any real purpose. Not everyone expected a fairy tale ending...just an ending that was justified. It seemed a little more bitter than sweet.<br /><br />Fifth: Despite everything that happened to Katniss and Peeta, and they both suffered terribly, and despite her finding some brief moments of comfort with Gale, this was always about her and Peeta. Gale's romantic involvement in Mockingjay only added unsatisfactory melodrama to the story. Collins played the triangle game once too often and too long...it was getting tiresome. The open ended victory of the revolution and what form it took is not necessarily a critical failure of the novel...it does leave sufficient doubt and provoke some interesting discussion. But there were still too many plot contrivances and dead spots to propel the story forward...it just felt awkward and unrealistic.
B0074MZSWW|4.0|The unmistakable presence of giant musical ghosts from the past haunt this record. The echoes are everywhere and they suggest the finest of pedigrees. Yet the Alabama Shakes have that extra dimension which is a key feature of all the contenders for the bands that somehow define the moment. They are hot, they are talked about in hushed tones and while there is nothing essentially new to be found on their debut \\"Boys and Girls\\" they have taken the familiar and infused it with the brilliant musical feat of making it sound new and original. Anyone listening to the superb debut single \\"Hold on\\" will be immediately struck by the homage to the soulful southern stew once concocted in that legendary studio Muscle Shoals in a small northwest corner of the bands home state. Here Wilson Pickett, Aretha Franklin, Percy Sledge and Bobby Womack created a distinct sound that famous bands like the Rolling Stones and more latterly the Black Keys have sought to replicate. This mix of rock and soul is all over \\"Boys and Girls\\" like a rash and the names Otis Redding and Janis Joplin are equally obvious influences. Add to this potent force that the Alabama Shakes have a primary weapon, a sort of musical nuclear option in the form of vocalist Brittany Howard a veritable force of nature and star in the making. Stop what your doing and check out the band on the Net at their now legendary Boston Arms gig in London in February 2012 and above all else seek out their cover of Led Zeppelin's \\"How many more times\\". You will be left speechless at the brazen energy which doesn't so much light up the stage as completely own it.<br /><br />Indeed if your reviewer has one complaint about this great debut album it doesn't quite press into the vinyl grooves the primal quality of the bands on stage presence and the sheer level of raucousness they summon up. Equally its an album that starts so strong that they set themselves an impossible standard to sustain over these eleven songs although to give them credit they try their damnedest. Inevitably you are drawn to the first two songs not least the superb single \\"Hold on\\". It is an instant classic, a song, which someone should have, written before and a performance by a band so tight you couldn't prize them apart. It is a rock 'n' soul standard in the making with its brilliant dirty sluggish riff and Howards sassily delivered opening line \\"Bless my heart, and bless my soul, didn't think I would make it, to twenty two years old\\". It is as if the Drive by Truckers have invited Otis Redding on stage for a jam and recorded it. The second song \\"I found you\\" is far more soulful and echoes the sort of Motown inspired laments that the late Amy Winehouse took to stratospheric levels on \\"Black to black\\". The song has a hook that could have carried on well beyond its meagre three minutes but the sign of a great band is to recognise the right level of economy and no song on this album tips over into the 5-minute territory. Further into the album you will also find a brilliant soul ballad \\"You ain't alone\\" which proves that Howard has a talent which should be celebrated and nurtured. If she is this good now what the future holds could be remarkable. Sadly the rest of the album doesn't quite reach the heights of these three songs. The short \\"Going to a party\\" is slightly pointless bearing in mind the songs the band has in their extensive live armoury. The penultimate song \\"I ain't the same\\" is fine but has been done many times before. Yet you can forgive them these missteps when you hear the fine conclusion to this album the much rockier \\"On your way\\" which is a splendid way to finish.<br /><br />Bearing in mind the sheer level of outlandish hyperbole around the Alabama Shakes in the US it may be the case that Boys and Girls was destined never to meet this weight of expectation. Nevertheless whilst this is no rock masterpiece it is a great debut. You also feel that once they are able to capture the sheer power of their live concerts in the recording studio then the Alabama Shakes will just about blow anything else off the stage. The enticing prospect they conjure up is truly delicious.s in the recording studio then the Alabama Shakes will just about blow anything else off the stage. The enticing prospect they conjure up is truly delicious.
